The expense allowance for troops on duty-related absence from place of employment was improved, together with travel subsidies and provisions for military service damaged soldiers and their families.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup> In addition, the position of non-commissioned officers was improved.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup></p>
<p>A women's policy machinery at the national level was established in 1972<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup> while amnesty was guaranteed in minor offences connected with demonstrations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Winkler_Sager_67-5"><a href="#cite_note-Winkler_Sager-67">[67]</a></sup> From 1970 onwards, parents as well as landlords were no longer legally prohibited “to give or rent rooms or flats to unmarried couples or to allow them to stay overnight.”<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup> In October 1972, the legal aid system was improved with the compensation paid to private lawyers for legal services to the poor increased.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup> The Bausparkassen Act of 1972<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup> placed all Bausparkassen (from January 1974 onwards) under the supervision of the Federal Banking Supervisory Office, and confined Bausparkassen “to the contract saving business and related activities.”<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> The Animal Protection Act, passed in 1972, introduced various safeguards for animals such as not permitting the causing of pain, injury, or suffering to an animal without justification, and limiting experiments to the minimum number of animals necessary.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup> In 1971, rules were introduced making it possible for former guestworkers “to receive an unlimited residence permit after a five-year stay.”<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup></p>
<p>Legislation aimed at safeguarding consumers was also implemented under the Brandt Administration. The consumer's right of withdrawal in case of hire purchase was strengthened in March 1974,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sophienschule.de_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-sophienschule.de-108">[108]</a></sup> and fixed prices for branded products were abolished by law in January that same year, which meant that manufacturers' recommended prices were not binding for retailers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sophienschule.de_108-1"><a href="#cite_note-sophienschule.de-108">[108]</a></sup> In addition, a progressive anticartel law was passed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Binder_18-4"><a href="#cite_note-Binder-18">[18]</a></sup> A 1969 law on explosive materials was supplemented by two orders; the first (made in November 1969) establishing a committee of experts for explosive materials, while the second order (made the following month) included details for the implementation of the law on explosive materials. An Act of December 1959 on the peaceful use of nuclear energy and protection against its dangers was amended by an Act of June 1970 that established a tax levied for the costs for permissions and surveillance measures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aei.pitt.edu_73-1"><a href="#cite_note-aei.pitt.edu-73">[73]</a></sup> The Law on Compensation for Measures of Criminal Prosecution and Penalties, passed in March 1971, provided for standardized compensation in certain situations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup> In addition, the budget for communications was increased.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_40-2"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-40">[40]</a></sup></p>
<p>In terms of working conditions, a number of reforms were introduced aimed at strengthening the rights of workers both at home and in the workplace. The Sickness Act of 1970 provided equal treatment of workers and employees in the event of incapacity for work,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bpb.de_61-1"><a href="#cite_note-bpb.de-61">[61]</a></sup> while maternity leave was increased.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup> Legislation was introduced in 1970 which ensured continued payment of wages for workers disabled by illness.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-willy-brandt.org_54-1"><a href="#cite_note-willy-brandt.org-54">[54]</a></sup> In 1970 all employees unit for work (with the exception of women in receipt of maternity benefits and temporarily and inconsiderably employed persons) were provided with an unconditional legal claim against their employer to continued payment of their gross wage for a period of 6 weeks, as also in the case of spa treatment approved by an Insurance Fund, the Fund bearing the full cost thereof. Previously, payment of employer's supplement and sick pay were only made from the day on which the doctor certified unfitness for work.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Surveyof_30-2"><a href="#cite_note-Surveyof-30">[30]</a></sup> In 1972, an Act on Agency Work was passed which sought to prevent works agencies from providing job placement services and aimed to provide job minimum protection for employees in agency work.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup> A law on the hiring out of manpower, passed in October 1972, contained provisions to stipulate prior authorization for the hiring out of manpower, to draw a distinction between the system governing workers hired out and the placing of workers, to regulate and improve the rights of hired out workers pertaining to working conditions and social insurance, and provide for more severe penalties and fines to be imposed on offenders.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aei.pitt_36-6"><a href="#cite_note-aei.pitt-36">[36]</a></sup></p>
<p>Improvements were also made in income and work conditions for home workers,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup> accident insurance was extended to non-working adults,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-socsci_22-2"><a href="#cite_note-socsci-22">[22]</a></sup> and the Border Zone Assistance Act (1971) increased levels of assistance to the declining zonal peripheral area.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup> The Occupational Safety Act (1973) required employers to provide company doctors and safety experts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup> A directive on protection against noise at the place of work was adopted in November 1970. If measurements showed or there was reason to assume that a noise level guide value of 90 dB( A) may be exceeded at the place of work, then the authority had to instruct the employer to arrange check-ups of the employees concerned and these employees had to use personal noise protection devices.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aei.pitt_36-7"><a href="#cite_note-aei.pitt-36">[36]</a></sup> A matching fund program for 15 million employees was also introduced, which stimulated them to accumulate capital.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Binder_18-5"><a href="#cite_note-Binder-18">[18]</a></sup></p>
<p>A ministerial order of January 1970 extended protection in cases of partial unemployment to home workers, while an ordinance of August 1970 fixed the conditions of health necessary for service in the merchant navy. A general provision of October 1970 determined in detail the circumstances in which the competent authority must take action on the basis of the act on the technical means of work. The requirement also stipulated the extent to which the technical standards established by national and international organisations can be regarded as “rules of the art.”<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aei.pitt.edu_73-2"><a href="#cite_note-aei.pitt.edu-73">[73]</a></sup> In a directive of 10 November 1970, the Minister of Labour and Social Affairs recommended to the higher authorities for work protection of the "Lander" to bring in the directive published, in agreement with the Ministry of Labour, by the German Engineers' Association on the evaluation of work station noise in relation to loss of hearing, in order to improve safeguards for workers against the noises in question. In September 1971, an ordinance was published concerning dangerous working materials; safeguarding persons using these materials against the dangers involved. In August 1971, a law came into force directed at reducing atmospheric pollution from lead compounds in four-stroke engine fuels. As a safeguard against radiation, a decree on the system of authorisations for medicaments treated with ionizing radiation or containing radioactive substances, in its version of 8 August 1967, was remodelled by a new Decree of 10 May 1971 which added some radionuclides to the list of medicaments which doctors in private practice were authorized to use.</p>
<p>By a decree of the Federal Minister for Labour and Social Order, the Federal Institute for Industrial Protection became the Federal Agency for Industrial Protection and Accident Research. Amongst its designated tasks included the promotion of industrial protection, accident prevention on the journey to and from work and accident prevention in the home and leisure activities, the encouragement of training and advanced training in the area of industrial protection, and to promote and coordinate accident research. A regulation was issued in 1972 which permitted for the first time the employment of women as drivers of trams, omnibuses and lorries, while further regulations laid down new provisions for lifts and work with compressed air.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aei.pitt_36-8"><a href="#cite_note-aei.pitt-36">[36]</a></sup> The Factory Constitution Law (1971) strengthened the rights of individual employees "to be informed and to be heard on matters concerning their place of work." The Works Council was provided with greater authority while trade unions were given the right of entry into the factory "provided they informed the employer of their intention to do so,"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Childs_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-Childs-13">[13]</a></sup> while a law was passed to encourage wider share ownership by workers and other rank-and-file employees.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Childs_13-2"><a href="#cite_note-Childs-13">[13]</a></sup> The Industrial Relations Law (1972) and the Personnel Representation Act (1974) broadened the rights of employees in matters which immediately affected their places of work, while also improving the possibilities for codetermination on operations committees, together with access of trade unions to companies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-willy-brandt.org_54-2"><a href="#cite_note-willy-brandt.org-54">[54]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Works Constitution Act of 1972 required in cases of collective dismissal at an establishment normally employing more than twenty employees that management and the works council must negotiate a social plan that stipulates compensation for workers who lose their jobs. In cases where the two parties could not agree on a social plan, the law provided for binding arbitration.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup> In 1972, the rights of works councils to information from management were not only strengthened, but works councils were also provided with full codetermination rights on issues such as working time arrangements in the plant, the setting of piece rates, plant wage systems, the establishment of vacation times, work breaks, overtime, and short-time work.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup> Legislation was passed which acknowledged for the first time the presence of trade unions in the workplace, expanded the means of action of the works councils, and improved their work basics as well as those of the youth councils.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated8_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated8-119">[119]</a></sup></p>
<p>A law of January 1972 on the organization of labour in enterprises significantly extended the works council's right of cooperation and co-management in the matter of vocational training. That same year, the Safety Institute of the Federal Republic of Germany was transformed into a public Federal Agency (Bundesanstalt) with significantly enlarged powers, in the context of which special emphasis would be placed on its new task of promoting and coordinating research in the area of accident prevention.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aei.pitt_36-9"><a href="#cite_note-aei.pitt-36">[36]</a></sup> New provisions were introduced for the rehabilitation of severely disabled people ("Schwerbehinderte") and accident victims.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MyLife_59-5"><a href="#cite_note-MyLife-59">[59]</a></sup> The Severely Disabled Persons Act of April 1974 obliged all employers with more than fifteen employees to ensure that 6% of their workforce consisted of people officially recognised as being severely handicapped. Employers who failed to do so were assessed 100 DM per month for every job falling before the required quota. These compensatory payments were used to "subsidise the adaptation of workplaces to the requirements of those who were severely handicapped."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Walker_26-3"><a href="#cite_note-Walker-26">[26]</a></sup></p>
<p>A law passed in January 1974, designed to protect members of the supervisory boards of companies who are undergoing training, was aimed at ensuring that the representatives of young workers and youthful members of works councils still undergoing training could perform their duties with greater independence and without fear of disadvantageous consequences for their future careers. On request, workers' representatives on completion of their training courses had to have an employment relationship of unlimited duration.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aei.pitt_c_47-2"><a href="#cite_note-aei.pitt_c-47">[47]</a></sup> In the field of transport, the Municipal Transportation Finance Law of 1971 established federal guidelines for subsidies to municipal governments,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup> while the Federal Transport Plan of 1973 provided a framework for all transport, including public transport.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup> In addition, the Severely Handicapped Persons Act of April 1974 extended the welfare and promotional obligations of the employer, and provided a right to extra holiday consisting of six working days.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_80-1"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-80">[80]</a></sup></p>
<p>A federal environmental programme was established in 1971,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup> and in 1972 laws were passed to regulate garbage elimination and air pollution via emission.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup> Matching grants covering 90% of infrastructure development were allocated to local communities, which led to a dramatic increase in the number of public swimming pools and other facilities of consumptive infrastructure throughout West Germany.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sinn_20-4"><a href="#cite_note-Sinn-20">[20]</a></sup> The federal crime-fighting apparatus was also modernised,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Binder_18-6"><a href="#cite_note-Binder-18">[18]</a></sup> while a Foreign Tax Act was passed which limited the possibility of tax evasion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup> In addition, efforts were made to improve the railways and motorways.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Binder_18-7"><a href="#cite_note-Binder-18">[18]</a></sup> In 1971, a law was passed setting the maximum lead content at 0.4 grams per liter of gasoline,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup> and in 1972 DDT was banned.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup> The Federal Immissions Control Law, passed in March 1974, provided protection from noxious gases, noise, and air-borne particulate matter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup></p>
<p>Under the Brandt Administration, West Germany attained a lower rate of inflation than in other industrialised countries at that time,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Prittie_17-9"><a href="#cite_note-Prittie-17">[17]</a></sup> while a rise in the standard of living took place, helped by the floating and revaluation of the mark.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Prittie_17-10"><a href="#cite_note-Prittie-17">[17]</a></sup> This was characterised by the real incomes of employees increasing more sharply than incomes from entrepreneurial work, with the proportion of employees' incomes in the overall national income rising from 65% to 70% between 1969 and 1973, while the proportion of income from entrepreneurial work and property fell over that same period from just under 35% to 30%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated7_38-3"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated7-38">[38]</a></sup> In addition, the percentage of West Germans living in poverty (based on various definitions) fell between 1969 and 1973.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup> According to one estimate, the percentage of West Germans living in poverty fell from 9.7% to 8.9% between 1969 and 1973, and from 20.2% to 14.0% according to another estimate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup> According to another estimate, the percentage of West Germans living in poverty during this period fell from 2.7% to 1.4%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1972_crisis">1972 crisis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Willy_Brandt&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: 1972 crisis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Brandt's <i>Ostpolitik</i> led to a meltdown of the narrow majority Brandt's coalition enjoyed in the <i>Bundestag</i>. In October 1970, FDP deputies <a href="/wiki/Erich_Mende" title="Erich Mende">Erich Mende</a>, Heinz Starke, and Siegfried Zoglmann crossed the floor to join the CDU. On 23 February 1972, SPD deputy <a href="/wiki/Herbert_Hupka" title="Herbert Hupka">Herbert Hupka</a>, who was also leader of the <i><a href="/wiki/Federation_of_Expellees" title="Federation of Expellees">Bund der Vertriebenen</a></i>, joined the CDU in disagreement with Brandt's reconciliatory efforts towards the east. On 23 April 1972, Wilhelm Helms (FDP) left the coalition ; the FDP politicians Knud von Kühlmann-Stumm and Gerhard Kienbaum also declared that they would vote against Brandt; thus, Brandt had lost his majority. On 24 April 1972 a vote of no confidence was proposed and it was voted on three days later. Had this motion passed, <a href="/wiki/Rainer_Barzel" title="Rainer Barzel">Rainer Barzel</a> would have replaced Brandt as chancellor. To everyone's surprise, the motion failed: Barzel got only 247 votes out of 260 ballots; for an absolute majority, 249 votes would have been necessary. There were also 10 votes against the motion and three invalid ballots. Most deputies of SPD and FDP did not take part in the voting, as not voting had the same effect as voting for Brandt.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="New_elections">New elections</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Willy_Brandt&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: New elections">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Though Brandt remained chancellor, he had lost his majority. Subsequent initiatives in parliament, most notably on the budget, failed. Because of this stalemate, the Bundestag was dissolved and new elections were called. During the 1972 campaign, many popular West German artists, intellectuals, writers, actors and professors supported Brandt and the SPD. Among them were <a href="/wiki/G%C3%BCnter_Grass" title="Günter Grass">Günter Grass</a>, <a href="/wiki/Walter_Jens" title="Walter Jens">Walter Jens</a>, and even the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Soccer" title="Soccer">soccer</a> player <a href="/wiki/Paul_Breitner" title="Paul Breitner">Paul Breitner</a>. Brandt's <i><span lang="de" xml:lang="de">Ostpolitik</span></i> as well as his reformist domestic policies were popular with parts of the young generation and he led the SPD to its best-ever federal election result in late 1972. The <i><span lang="de" xml:lang="de">Willy-Wahl</span></i>, Brandt's landslide win was the beginning of the end; and Brandt's role in government started to decline.</p>
<p>Many of Brandt's reforms met with resistance from state governments (dominated by CDU/CSU). The spirit of reformist optimism was cut short by the <a href="/wiki/1973_oil_crisis" title="1973 oil crisis">1973 oil crisis</a> and the major public services strike 1974, which gave Germany's trade unions, led by <a href="/wiki/Heinz_Kluncker" title="Heinz Kluncker">Heinz Kluncker</a>, a big wage increase but reduced Brandt's financial leeway for further reforms. Brandt was said to be more a dreamer than a manager and was personally haunted by depression. To counter any notions about being sympathetic to Communism or soft on left-wing extremists, Brandt implemented tough legislation that barred "radicals" from public service (<i><span lang="de" xml:lang="de"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radikalenerlass" title="Radikalenerlass">Radikalenerlass</a></span></i>).</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Guillaume_affair">Guillaume affair</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Willy_Brandt&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Guillaume affair">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Guillaume_Affair" title="Guillaume Affair">Guillaume Affair</a></div>
<p>Around 1973, West German security organizations [which ones?] received information that one of Brandt's personal assistants, <a href="/wiki/G%C3%BCnter_Guillaume" title="Günter Guillaume">Günter Guillaume</a>, was a spy for the East German intelligence services. Brandt was asked to continue working as usual, and he agreed to do so, even taking a private vacation with Guillaume. Guillaume was arrested on 24 April 1974, and many<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag possibly uses too-vague attribution or weasel words. (August 2015)">who?</span></a></i>]</sup> blamed Brandt for having a communist spy in his inner circle.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>Brandt resigned from his position as chancellor on 6 May 1974, but he remained member of the <a href="/wiki/Bundestag" title="Bundestag">Bundestag</a> and chairman of the Social Democrats through 1987. This <a href="/wiki/Espionage" title="Espionage">espionage</a> affair is widely considered to have been just the trigger for Brandt's resignation, not the fundamental cause. As Brandt himself later said, "I was exhausted, for reasons which had nothing to do with the affair [the Guillaume espionage scandal] going on at the time."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup> Brandt was dogged by scandals about serial adultery, and reportedly also struggled with alcohol and depression.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup> There was also the economic fallout on West Germany of the <a href="/wiki/1973_oil_crisis" title="1973 oil crisis">1973 oil crisis</a>, which may seem to have given enough stress to finish off Brandt as the Chancellor.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>Guillaume had been an espionage agent for <a href="/wiki/East_Germany" title="East Germany">East Germany</a>, who was supervised by <a href="/wiki/Markus_Wolf" title="Markus Wolf">Markus Wolf</a>, the head of the <a href="/wiki/Main_Directorate_for_Reconnaissance" title="Main Directorate for Reconnaissance">Main Directorate for Reconnaissance</a> (<i>Hauptverwaltung Aufklärung</i> or HVA—the foreign intelligence service) of the East German Ministry for State Security. Wolf stated after the reunification that the resignation of Brandt had never been intended, and that the planting and handling of Guillaume had been one of the largest mistakes of the East German secret services.</p>
<p>Brandt was succeeded as the Chancellor of the Bundesrepublik by his fellow Social Democrat, <a href="/wiki/Helmut_Schmidt" title="Helmut Schmidt">Helmut Schmidt</a>. For the rest of his life, Brandt remained suspicious that his fellow Social Democrat (and longtime rival) <a href="/wiki/Herbert_Wehner" title="Herbert Wehner">Herbert Wehner</a> had been scheming for Brandt's downfall.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> However, there is scant evidence to corroborate this suspicion.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Ex-Chancellor">Ex-Chancellor</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Willy_Brandt&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Ex-Chancellor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_B_145_Bild-F079282-0022,_M%C3%BCnster,_SPD-Parteitag,_Willy_Brandt.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="501" height="271" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Bundesarchiv_B_145_Bild-F079282-0022%2C_M%C3%BCnster%2C_SPD-Parteitag%2C_Willy_Brandt.jpg/170px-Bundesarchiv_B_145_Bild-F079282-0022%2C_M%C3%BCnster%2C_SPD-Parteitag%2C_Willy_Brandt.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Bundesarchiv_B_145_Bild-F079282-0022%2C_M%C3%BCnster%2C_SPD-Parteitag%2C_Willy_Brandt.jpg/255px-Bundesarchiv_B_145_Bild-F079282-0022%2C_M%C3%BCnster%2C_SPD-Parteitag%2C_Willy_Brandt.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Bundesarchiv_B_145_Bild-F079282-0022%2C_M%C3%BCnster%2C_SPD-Parteitag%2C_Willy_Brandt.jpg/340px-Bundesarchiv_B_145_Bild-F079282-0022%2C_M%C3%BCnster%2C_SPD-Parteitag%2C_Willy_Brandt.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_B_145_Bild-F079282-0022,_M%C3%BCnster,_SPD-Parteitag,_Willy_Brandt.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Willy Brandt in 1988 at the Münster party rally</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>After his term as the Chancellor, Brandt retained his seat in the <a href="/wiki/Bundestag" title="Bundestag">Bundestag</a>, and he remained the Chairman of the Social Democratic Party through 1987. Beginning in 1987, Brandt stepped down to become the Honorary Chairman of the party. Brandt was also a member of the <a href="/wiki/European_Parliament" title="European Parliament">European Parliament</a> from 1979 to 1983.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Socialist_International">Socialist International</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Willy_Brandt&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Socialist International">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:WillyBrandtBerntCarlssonPenttiV%C3%A4%C3%A4n%C3%A4nen.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2485" data-file-width="3546" height="154" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/WillyBrandtBerntCarlssonPenttiV%C3%A4%C3%A4n%C3%A4nen.jpg/220px-WillyBrandtBerntCarlssonPenttiV%C3%A4%C3%A4n%C3%A4nen.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/WillyBrandtBerntCarlssonPenttiV%C3%A4%C3%A4n%C3%A4nen.jpg/330px-WillyBrandtBerntCarlssonPenttiV%C3%A4%C3%A4n%C3%A4nen.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/WillyBrandtBerntCarlssonPenttiV%C3%A4%C3%A4n%C3%A4nen.jpg/440px-WillyBrandtBerntCarlssonPenttiV%C3%A4%C3%A4n%C3%A4nen.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:WillyBrandtBerntCarlssonPenttiV%C3%A4%C3%A4n%C3%A4nen.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Socialist_International" title="Socialist International">Socialist International</a> Congress 1983. Willy Brandt with outgoing general secretary <a href="/wiki/Bernt_Carlsson" title="Bernt Carlsson">Bernt Carlsson</a> (left) and new general secretary Pentti Väänänen (right)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>For sixteen years, Brandt was the president of the <a href="/wiki/Socialist_International" title="Socialist International">Socialist International</a> (1976–92), during which period the number of Socialist International's mainly European member parties grew until there were more than a hundred socialist, social democratic, and labour political parties around the world. For the first seven years, this growth in SI membership had been prompted by the efforts of the Socialist International's Secretary-General, the Swede <a href="/wiki/Bernt_Carlsson" title="Bernt Carlsson">Bernt Carlsson</a>. However, in early 1983, a dispute arose about what Carlsson perceived as the SI president's authoritarian approach. Carlsson then rebuked Brandt saying: "this is a Socialist International — not a German International".</p>
<p>Next, against some vocal opposition, Brandt decided to move the next Socialist International Congress from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sydney,_Australia" title="Sydney, Australia">Sydney, Australia</a> to <a href="/wiki/Portugal" title="Portugal">Portugal</a>. Following this SI Congress in April 1983, Brandt retaliated against Carlsson by forcing him to step down from his position. However, the <a href="/wiki/Austria" title="Austria">Austrian</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prime_Minister" title="Prime Minister">Prime Minister</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bruno_Kreisky" title="Bruno Kreisky">Bruno Kreisky</a>, argued on behalf of Brandt: "It is a question of whether it is better to be pure or to have greater numbers".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup> Carlsson was succeeded by the Finn Pentti Väänänen as Secretary General of the <a href="/wiki/Socialist_International" title="Socialist International">Socialist International</a> <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup></p>
<p>During Willy Brandt's presidency the SI developed activities and dialogue on a number of International issues. This concerned the East-West conflict and arms race where the SI held high level consultations with the leaderships of the United States and the Soviet Union. The SI met with such leaders as President <a href="/wiki/Jimmy_Carter" title="Jimmy Carter">Jimmy Carter</a> and Vice-Presidents <a href="/wiki/Walter_Mondale" title="Walter Mondale">Walter Mondale</a> and George Bush. They also met with the Secretaries General Leonid Brezhnev and <a href="/wiki/Mikhail_Gorbachev" title="Mikhail Gorbachev">Mikhail Gorbachev</a> and with the Soviet President Andrei Gromyko. The SI also developed active contacts to promote dialogue concerning regional conflicts. Those included the Middle East, where they helped to build contacts between Israel and the PLO, and also in Southern Africa and Central America.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Brandt_Report">Brandt Report</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Willy_Brandt&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Brandt Report">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 1977, Brandt was appointed as the chairman of the Independent Commission for International Developmental Issues. This produced a report in 1980, which called for drastic changes in the global attitude towards development in the Third World. This became known as the <a href="/wiki/Brandt_Report" title="Brandt Report">Brandt Report</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Reunification">Reunification</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Willy_Brandt&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Reunification">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-1990-0316-035,_Wismar,_SPD-Wahlkundgebung,_Willy_Brandt.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="567" data-file-width="800" height="156" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-1990-0316-035%2C_Wismar%2C_SPD-Wahlkundgebung%2C_Willy_Brandt.jpg/220px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-1990-0316-035%2C_Wismar%2C_SPD-Wahlkundgebung%2C_Willy_Brandt.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-1990-0316-035%2C_Wismar%2C_SPD-Wahlkundgebung%2C_Willy_Brandt.jpg/330px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-1990-0316-035%2C_Wismar%2C_SPD-Wahlkundgebung%2C_Willy_Brandt.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-1990-0316-035%2C_Wismar%2C_SPD-Wahlkundgebung%2C_Willy_Brandt.jpg/440px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-1990-0316-035%2C_Wismar%2C_SPD-Wahlkundgebung%2C_Willy_Brandt.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-1990-0316-035,_Wismar,_SPD-Wahlkundgebung,_Willy_Brandt.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Willy Brandt giving a speech in <a href="/wiki/Wismar" title="Wismar">Wismar</a>, campaigning for the <a href="/wiki/East_German_general_election,_1990" title="East German general election, 1990">only free parliamentary election in East Germany</a> (March 1990).</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In October 1979, Brandt met with the East German dissident, <a href="/wiki/Rudolf_Bahro" title="Rudolf Bahro">Rudolf Bahro</a>, who had written <i>The Alternative</i>. Bahro and his supporters were attacked by the East German state security organization <a href="/wiki/Stasi" title="Stasi">Stasi</a>, headed by <a href="/wiki/Erich_Mielke" title="Erich Mielke">Erich Mielke</a>, for his writings, which had laid the theoretical foundation of a leftist opposition to the ruling SED party and its dependent allies, and which promoted new and changed parties. All of this is now described as "change from within". Brandt had asked for Bahro's release, and Brandt welcomed Bahro's theories, which advanced the debate within his own Social Democratic Party. In late 1989, Brandt became one of the first leftwing leaders in West Germany to publicly favor a quick <a href="/wiki/German_reunification" title="German reunification">reunification</a> of Germany, instead of some sort of two-state federation or other kind of interim arrangement. Brandt's public statement "Now grows together what belongs together," was widely quoted in those days.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hostages_in_Iraq">Hostages in Iraq</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Willy_Brandt&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Hostages in Iraq">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>One of Brandt's last public appearances was in flying to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Baghdad,_Iraq" title="Baghdad, Iraq">Baghdad, Iraq</a>, to free Western hostages held by <a href="/wiki/Saddam_Hussein" title="Saddam Hussein">Saddam Hussein</a>, following the Iraqi invasion of <a href="/wiki/Kuwait" title="Kuwait">Kuwait</a> in 1990. Brandt secured the release of a large number of them, and on 9 November 1990, his <a href="/wiki/Airplane" title="Airplane">airplane</a> landed with 174 freed hostages on board at the <a href="/wiki/Frankfurt_Airport" title="Frankfurt Airport">Frankfurt Airport</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Death_and_memorials">Death and memorials</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Willy_Brandt&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Death and memorials">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Willy_Brandt_monument_in_Warsaw_05.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3001" data-file-width="4293" height="154" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Willy_Brandt_monument_in_Warsaw_05.JPG/220px-Willy_Brandt_monument_in_Warsaw_05.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Willy_Brandt_monument_in_Warsaw_05.JPG/330px-Willy_Brandt_monument_in_Warsaw_05.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Willy_Brandt_monument_in_Warsaw_05.JPG/440px-Willy_Brandt_monument_in_Warsaw_05.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Willy_Brandt_monument_in_Warsaw_05.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Willy_Brandt_Monument_(Warsaw)" title="Willy Brandt Monument (Warsaw)">Willy Brandt memorial</a> in <a href="/wiki/Warsaw" title="Warsaw">Warsaw</a>, 2000. Brandt´s memorial is within sight of the Warsaw Ghetto Memorial.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Willy_Brandt_Memorial-Nuremberg.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2214" data-file-width="2148" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Willy_Brandt_Memorial-Nuremberg.jpg/220px-Willy_Brandt_Memorial-Nuremberg.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Willy_Brandt_Memorial-Nuremberg.jpg/330px-Willy_Brandt_Memorial-Nuremberg.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Willy_Brandt_Memorial-Nuremberg.jpg/440px-Willy_Brandt_Memorial-Nuremberg.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Willy_Brandt_Memorial-Nuremberg.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Willy-Brandt-Memorial in <a href="/wiki/Nuremberg" title="Nuremberg">Nuremberg</a> by the artist <a href="/wiki/Josef_Tabachnyk" title="Josef Tabachnyk">Josef Tabachnyk</a>, 2009</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Willy Brandt died of <a href="/wiki/Colorectal_cancer" title="Colorectal cancer">colon cancer</a> at his home in <a href="/wiki/Unkel" title="Unkel">Unkel</a>, a town on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rhine_River" title="Rhine River">Rhine River</a>, on 8 October 1992, and was given a <a href="/wiki/State_funeral" title="State funeral">state funeral</a>. He was buried at the cemetery at <a href="/wiki/Zehlendorf_(Berlin)" title="Zehlendorf (Berlin)">Zehlendorf</a> in Berlin.</p>
<p>The Federal Chancellor Willy Brandt Foundation was erected in 1994. It serves to honor the memory of Brandt's political accomplishents and his commitment to peace, freedom and democracy. The foundation runs two permanent exhibitions – one in Berlin, and the other in Lübeck, where Brandt was born. Other works of the foundation include the edition of Brandt's papers, speeches and letters (the Berlin Edition), historical research as well as organizing lectures and international conferences.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup></p>
<p>When the SPD moved its headquarters from Bonn back to Berlin in the mid-1990s, the new headquarters was named the "Willy Brandt Haus". One of the buildings of the <a href="/wiki/Espace_L%C3%A9opold" title="Espace Léopold">European Parliament in Brussels</a> was named after him in 2008.</p>
<p>On 6 December 2000, a <a href="/wiki/Willy_Brandt_Monument_(Warsaw)" title="Willy Brandt Monument (Warsaw)">memorial to Willy Brandt</a> and <i><a href="/wiki/Warschauer_Kniefall" title="Warschauer Kniefall">Warschauer Kniefall</a></i> was unveiled in <a href="/wiki/Warsaw" title="Warsaw">Warsaw</a>, Poland.</p>
<p>German artist <a href="/wiki/Johannes_Heisig" title="Johannes Heisig">Johannes Heisig</a> painted several portraits of Brandt of which one was unveiled as part of an honoring event at German Historical Institute Washington, DC on 18 March 2003. Spokesmen amongst others were former German Federal Minister Egon Bahr and former U.S. Secretary of state Henry Kissinger.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GHI_139-0"><a href="#cite_note-GHI-139">[139]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2009, the Willy-Brandt-Memorial was opened up in Nuremberg at the Willy-Brandt Square.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup> It was created by the artist <a href="/wiki/Josef_Tabachnyk" title="Josef Tabachnyk">Josef Tabachnyk</a>.</p>
<p>In 2009, the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Erfurt" title="University of Erfurt">University of Erfurt</a> renamed its <a href="/wiki/Graduate_school" title="Graduate school">graduate school</a> of <a href="/wiki/Public_administration" title="Public administration">public administration</a> as the Willy Brandt School of Public Policy. A <a href="/wiki/Private_school" title="Private school">private</a> German-language <a href="/wiki/Secondary_school" title="Secondary school">secondary school</a> in <a href="/wiki/Warsaw" title="Warsaw">Warsaw</a>, Poland, is also named after Brandt.</p>
<p>The main boulevard on the north entrance to <a href="/wiki/Montenegro" title="Montenegro">Montenegrin</a> capital <a href="/wiki/Podgorica" title="Podgorica">Podgorica</a> was named Willy Brandt Boulevard in 2011.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup></p>
<p>Willy Brandt also has an unusual memorial in <a href="/wiki/Hammersmith" title="Hammersmith">Hammersmith</a> in <a href="/wiki/London" title="London">London</a>, United Kingdom. In 1963, when he was Mayor of West Berlin, Brandt travelled to Hammersmith with a <a href="/wiki/Street_light" title="Street light">street lamp</a> from West Berlin, and presented it to the Mayor of Hammersmith to mark its twinning with <a href="/wiki/Neuk%C3%B6lln" title="Neukölln">Neukölln</a>. The lamp now stands on the wall of <a href="/wiki/Westcott_Lodge" title="Westcott Lodge">Westcott Lodge</a>, facing <a href="/wiki/Furnival_Gardens" title="Furnival Gardens">Furnival Gardens</a>, with a commemorative plaque below it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Brandt.27s_family">Brandt's family</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Willy_Brandt&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Brandt's family">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Unreferenced" role="presentation">
<tr>
<td class="mbox-image">
<div style="width:52px"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="399" data-file-width="512" height="39" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" width="50"/></a></div>
</td>
<td class="mbox-text"><span class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>does not <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources">cite</a> any <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">sources</a></b>. <span class="hide-when-compact">Please help <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Willy_Brandt&amp;action=edit">improve this section</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Introduction_to_referencing_with_Wiki_Markup/1" title="Help:Introduction to referencing with Wiki Markup/1">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability#Burden_of_evidence" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">removed</a>.</span> <small><i>(October 2013)</i></small> <small class="hide-when-compact"><i>(<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></span></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>From 1941 until 1948 Brandt was married to Anna Carlotta Thorkildsen (the daughter of a Norwegian father and a German-American mother). They had a daughter, Ninja Brandt (born in 1940). After Brandt and Thorkildsen were divorced in 1948, Brandt married the Norwegian-born German writer <a href="/wiki/Rut_Brandt" title="Rut Brandt">Rut Hansen</a> in the same year. Hansen and Brandt had three sons: <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Peter_Brandt&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Peter Brandt (page does not exist)">Peter Brandt</a><span class="noprint" style="font-size:85%; font-style: normal;"> (<a class="extiw" href="https://de.wikipedia.org/wiki/Peter_Brandt_(Historiker)" title="de:Peter Brandt (Historiker)">de</a>)</span> (born in 1948), <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Lars_Brandt_(author)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Lars Brandt (author) (page does not exist)">Lars Brandt</a><span class="noprint" style="font-size:85%; font-style: normal;"> (<a class="extiw" href="https://de.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lars_Brandt" title="de:Lars Brandt">de</a>)</span> (born in 1951) and <a href="/wiki/Matthias_Brandt" title="Matthias Brandt">Matthias Brandt</a> (born in 1961). After 32 years of marriage, Willy Brandt and Rut Hansen Brand divorced in 1980, and from the day that they were divorced they never saw each other again. On 9 December 1983, Brandt married <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Brigitte_Seebacher&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Brigitte Seebacher (page does not exist)">Brigitte Seebacher</a><span class="noprint" style="font-size:85%; font-style: normal;"> (<a class="extiw" href="https://de.wikipedia.org/wiki/Brigitte_Seebacher" title="de:Brigitte Seebacher">de</a>)</span> (born in 1946).</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Selected_works">Selected works</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Willy_Brandt&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Selected works">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<ul>
<li>1960 <i>Mein Weg nach Berlin</i> (<i>My Path to Berlin</i>), autobiography</li>
<li>1966 <i>Draußen. Schriften während der Emigration.</i> (<i>Outside: Writings during the Emigration</i>) <a class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn" href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/3801210944">ISBN 3-8012-1094-4</a></li>
<li>1968 <i>Friedenspolitik in Europa</i> (<i>The Politics of Peace in Europe</i>)</li>
<li>1976 <i>Begegnungen und Einsichten 1960–1975</i> (<i>Encounters and Insights 1960–1975</i>) <a class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn" href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/3455089798">ISBN 3-455-08979-8</a></li>
<li>1982 <i>Links und frei. Mein Weg 1930–1950</i> (<i>Left and Free: My Path 1930–1950</i>)</li>
<li>1986 <i>Der organisierte Wahnsinn</i> (<i>Organized Lunacy</i>)</li>
<li>1989 <i>Erinnerungen</i> (<i>Memories</i>) <a class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn" href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/3549073534">ISBN 3-549-07353-4</a></li>
<li>2002 Berliner Ausgabe, Werkauswahl, ed. for Bundeskanzler Willy Brandt Stiftung by <a href="/wiki/Helga_Grebing" title="Helga Grebing">Helga Grebing</a>, Gregor Schöllgen and Heinrich August Winkler, 10 volumes, Dietz Verlag, Bonn 2002f, Collected Writings, <a class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn" href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/3801203050">ISBN 3-8012-0305-0</a></li>
</ul>

<tr>
<th class="navbox-group" scope="row">1901–1925</th>
<td class="navbox-list navbox-odd" style="text-align:left;border-left-width:2px;border-left-style:solid;width:100%;padding:0px">
<div style="padding:0em 0.25em">
<ul>
<li><span style="font-size:90%;">1901</span> <a href="/wiki/Henry_Dunant" title="Henry Dunant">Henry Dunant</a> / <a href="/wiki/Fr%C3%A9d%C3%A9ric_Passy" title="Frédéric Passy">Frédéric Passy</a></li>
<li><span style="font-size:90%;">1902</span> <a href="/wiki/%C3%89lie_Ducommun" title="Élie Ducommun">Élie Ducommun</a> / <a href="/wiki/Charles_Albert_Gobat" title="Charles Albert Gobat">Charles Gobat</a></li>
<li><span style="font-size:90%;">1903</span> <a href="/wiki/Randal_Cremer" title="Randal Cremer">Randal Cremer</a></li>
<li><span style="font-size:90%;">1904</span> <a href="/wiki/Institut_de_Droit_International" title="Institut de Droit International">Institut de Droit International</a></li>
<li><span style="font-size:90%;">1905</span> <a href="/wiki/Bertha_von_Suttner" title="Bertha von Suttner">Bertha von Suttner</a></li>
<li><span style="font-size:90%;">1906</span> <a href="/wiki/Theodore_Roosevelt" title="Theodore Roosevelt">Theodore Roosevelt</a></li>
